tool
1	"""PyTorch reference for a Kimi-Linear W4A16 hybrid decode unit (batch=1).
2	
3	Correctness oracle, NOT the fast baseline (baseline.py) or the SOTA ceiling.
4	One repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B -- 3 KDA (gated-delta linear attn)
5	layers + 1 MLA (multi-head latent attention) layer, each + a 64-expert MoE FFN
6	-- decoded one token at a time, with the big projection weights stored as
7	**W4A16** (int4 weights, group-128 asymmetric, bf16 accumulation; the AWQ/GPTQ
8	format that open-source inference actually ships).
9	
10	Why W4A16 and not fp8: at batch-1 decode the bottleneck is *weight memory
11	traffic*. int4 weights are 0.25x the bytes of bf16 (4x less traffic), and a
12	fused dequant-GEMV realizes that win in the memory-bound regime where decode
… +360 more lines

[Read] /ephemeral/mega-outputs/runs/20260716_091411_kinetic-claude_kinetic-0715_02_kimi_linear_decode/repo/problems/02_kimi_linear_decode/baseline.py
tool
1	"""Optimized-PyTorch W4A16 baseline for the Kimi-Linear decode unit.
2	
3	The floor the solution must beat -- NOT the oracle (reference.py). Same int4
4	weights, same math, written the competent pure-PyTorch way: bf16 accumulation
5	and batched MoE expert dequant instead of the reference's fp32 per-expert loop.
6	
7	It still *materializes* each dequantized bf16 weight before the matmul (int4
8	read + bf16 write + bf16 read = ~9x the traffic of a fused path). That is the
9	whole point left on the table: stock PyTorch cannot fuse the int4 unpack into
10	the GEMV, so beating this baseline requires a custom fused dequant-GEMV kernel.
11	`score = baseline_latency / solution_latency`.
12	"""

[Read] /ephemeral/mega-outputs/runs/20260716_091411_kinetic-claude_kinetic-0715_02_kimi_linear_decode/repo/problems/02_kimi_linear_decode/check.py
tool
1	"""Correctness runner for the Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit.
2	
3	Per-step correctness, not a chained trajectory: for each seed and a couple of
4	context lengths, the reference and the solution are given an IDENTICAL fed
5	state and one new token, each runs ONE decode step, and we compare the
6	next-token hidden plus the updated decode state (KDA recurrent state, MLA latent
7	cache) by cosine similarity. Cosine sim is robust to bf16/fp8 precision but
8	still rejects wrong answers. Chaining steps would just measure how fast two
9	precisions diverge, which is not a kernel-correctness signal.
10	"""
11	from __future__ import annotations

11	"""

1	"""Decode shapes for the Kimi-Linear hybrid unit.
2	
3	Score is latency speedup over baseline.py, so the sweep is over decode context

5	KDA state + compressed MLA latent cache) pays off and where the absorb-vs-
6	materialize fork in MLA decides the whole thing -- that is the shippable "wow".
7	
8	`n_experts` is 64 for v0 (real Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B is 256); bump it once the
9	problem is calibrated. `steps` is how many tokens to decode autoregressively
10	when timing (fewer at long context to bound wall time).
11	"""

reasoning
Baseline: ~8.0/8.3/9.2 ms per token. The theoretical floor from weight streaming is 0.59 ms (1.07GB at 1.8TB/s). Well, at batch 1 with 188 SMs, achievable bandwidth might be ~1.4-1.6 TB/s in practice → ~0.7-0.8 ms. Huge headroom: potential 10x speedup. Let me now carefully analyze the compute: Model: 4 blocks. Each block: - RMSNorm (hidden=2304) - Attention (3× KDA, 1× MLA) - RMSNorm - MoE (64 experts, top-8 + 1 shared, inter=1024) Weight bytes per block: KDA layer: - q_proj, k_proj, v_proj, g_proj: QuantLinear(2304 → 4096): w_q (1152, 4096) bytes = 4.718M bytes each; scales/zeros (18, 4096) bf16 = 147KB each. ~4.87MB each → 4 × ~4.87 = ~19.5MB - beta_proj: nn.Linear(2304→32) bf16 = 147KB - conv_w: (3, 4096, 4) bf16 = 98KB - o_proj: QuantLinear(4096→2304): w_q (2048, 2304) = 4.718MB + scales (32,2304)*2*2... ≈ 4.87MB Total KDA ≈ 24.6MB MLA layer: - q_proj: QuantLinear(2304 → 32*192=6144): w_q (1152,6144) = 7.08MB - kv_a: QuantLinear(2304 → 512+64=576): (1152,576)=0.663MB - kv_b: QuantLinear(512 → 32*256=8192): (256, 8192) = 2.097MB - o_proj: QuantLinear(4096 → 2304): (2048,2304)=4.72MB Total MLA ≈ 14.6MB + scales/zeros minor MoE per block (64 experts + 1 shared): - gate/up: QuantExperts(64, 2304, 1024): w_q (64, 1152, 1024) = 75.5MB each - down: QuantExperts(64, 1024, 2304): w_q (64, 512, 2304) = 75.5MB Total routed experts: 3×75.5 = 226.5MB, but only top-8 are used per token → 8/64 of that = 28.3MB/token - shared expert: 3× (1152,1024)/... = ~3.5MB - router: 64×2304 bf16 = 0.29MB So per-token traffic ≈ 3×24.6 (KDA) + 14.6 (MLA) + 4×(28.3+3.5+0.3) (MoE) ≈ 73.8 + 14.6 + 128.4 ≈ 217MB/token?? Wait that doesn't match 1.07GB total. Let me recompute total weights: 4 blocks × (3×(24.6/4... no. Hold on: total = 4 blocks × (attn + moe) where attn is KDA for 3 blocks and MLA for 1: - KDA attn per block ≈ 24.6MB × 3 blocks = 73.8MB - MLA attn ≈ 14.6MB × 1 = 14.6MB - MoE per block: ALL experts = 226.5+3.5 (shared) + 0.3 (router) = 230.3MB × 4 blocks = 921MB Total ≈ 73.8+14.6+921 = 1009MB ≈ 1.01GB + scales/zeros ≈ 1.07GB ✓. Per-token streamed bytes: all attn weights (88.4MB) + for MoE only the 9 active experts (8 routed+1 shared) per block: 4×(9×3.54MB + 0.3) ≈ 4×35.2 = 127.7MB... wait per expert per weight type: gate (1152,1024)=1.18MB, up 1.18MB, down (512,2304)=1.18MB → 3.54MB per expert. 9 experts × 3.54 = 31.9MB per block + router 0.3MB → ~32.2MB × 4 = 128.7MB. Plus scales/zeros for active experts: scales (18,1024) bf16 = 36.9KB each, negligible. Total per-token ≈ 88.4 + 128.7 ≈ 217MB. At 1.8TB/s → 120 µs; at realistic 1.4TB/s → 155µs → ~6000 tok/s. vs baseline 125 tok/s → up to ~50x theoretical. That would be amazing but realistically we should target maybe 10-20x. BUT the constraint: single megakernel launch. This is extremely challenging for a full forward in one kernel because there are many dependent stages: Per block: 1. RMSNorm(x) 2. attn projections (depend on normalized x) 3. KDA: conv, recurrence S update, o_proj 4. MLA: roped q/k, append cache, kv_b, attention over ctx, o_proj 5. residual add 6. RMSNorm(h) 7. MoE: router, top-8, per-expert GEMVs, accumulation 8. residual add All of this has strict sequential dependencies within a token. In a single kernel launch we must use grid-wide synchronization between stages (or a persistent-CTA design with cooperative groups / grid.sync(), or spin-wait on atomics in global memory). Options for grid sync in a single launch: - CUDA cooperative groups grid.sync() — requires launch via cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel; all blocks resident. With torch load_inline, we can call cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel ourselves. Grid size limited to max resident blocks (188 SMs × blocks/SM). - Triton: no grid sync. Could emulate with atomics spin-wait — risky but doable. Triton's `tl.atomic_add` etc. can implement a barrier counter with spinning loops. Actually there's another approach that avoids grid sync entirely: use a SINGLE thread block (grid=1) for the whole forward?? That would leave 187 SMs idle — GEMV bandwidth would be terrible. A single SM can pull maybe 30-50 GB/s... too slow. Or: all parallelism in one dimension with atomics & spin barriers. This is what "megakernel" solutions typically do: grid-wide barrier via atomic counter + volatile polling, or better, arrive-and-wait on two counters alternating phase. CUDA C with load_inline and cooperative launch is the natural choice. But one subtlety: with cooperative launch, grid size must fit in residency: 188 SMs × N blocks per SM. If we use e.g. 256 threads/block and just 188 blocks (1/SM), we have 188×256 = 48128 threads. For a GEMV of 4096 outputs... fine. But really we want max memory bandwidth. GEMV at batch-1: each output = dot(x, W[:,n]). Total FLOPs tiny; we want to read the weight with 128-bit loads across all SMs. int4 GEMV: read uint8 packed (or better uint32/uint4 vectors), dequant, fma with x broadcast. Typically each block handles a set of output columns; threads cooperate along k using vectorized loads (uint4 = 16 bytes = 32 int4s). Now let me design the megakernel carefully. ## Architecture plan ### Key facts - hidden d = 2304 (18 groups of 128) - KDA: H=32, Dk=Dv=128, conv kernel 4 (window 3 prev + current) - MLA: H=32, kv_lora=512, qk_nope=128, qk_rope=64, v_head=128; scale = 192^-0.5; rope theta 10000 on 64-dim (32 freqs) - MoE: E=64, top-8, shared 1, inter m=1024, routed_scaling=2.446 - 4 blocks: [K,K,K,M], each with MoE ### Precision plan (matching reference within cos ≥ 0.98) Reference computes lots in fp32 then casts to bf16 at GEMV inputs/outputs. To be safe: - RMSNorm: fp32 internally (mean of squares 2304 → exact), output bf16 (reference `.to(x.dtype)` where x is bf16 → bf16 out). Match: norm output bf16. Actually reference _rmsnorm returns bf16, then QuantLinear does x.float() @ w.float(). bf16 input → exact same products as if we dequant to fp32... Reference GEMV: x.float() @ w_bf.float() in fp32 then → bf16. Our kernel: dequant w into fp32 regs, x bf16→fp32, fp32 FMA accumulation → match closely. bf16 rounding at the end ok. - KDA internals: q,k,v after conv are silu(fp32 sums)→bf16 (conv output cast to val.dtype=bf16). Then q,k,v → float for state update. g = -softplus(g_proj) computed from fp32 g_proj output (g_proj returns bf16! `self.g_proj(x)` → bf16, then .float()). beta: bf16 Linear → sigmoid in float. - S update in fp32; o = S^T q in fp32 → bf16 → o_proj. - MLA: q from q_proj bf16; rope in fp32, cast bf16 (q_rope after rope is `.float()` kept fp32! `q_rope = _apply_rope(q_rope, cos, sin).float()` — _apply_rope casts to bf16 (x.dtype) then .float() → so bf16-rounded values as fp32. k_rope: _apply_rope → bf16 (stored into cache as bf16). c_kv bf16. - scores: q_nope(fp32 from bf16) · k_nope where k_nope = kvb (fp32! `kvb = ... .float()` — kv_b output bf16 → viewed → float). In reference (not baseline): kvb = self.kv_b(st["c_kv"]).view(...).float() — kv_b returns bf16 (fp32 acc then cast). So k_nope/v are bf16-rounded then fp32-used. - scores fp32, softmax fp32 (dim=0 over L), p fp32. o = einsum("lh,lhd->hd", p, v) fp32 → reshape → bf16 → o_proj. - Absorbed attention equivalence: reference materializes k_nope[l,h,d] = Σ_r c_kv[l,r]·W_knope[r, h*256+d] (bf16 rounding after fp32 GEMM over r!) then score = Σ_d q_nope[h,d]·k_nope[l,h,d] fp32. Absorbed: score = Σ_r c_kv[l,r] · (Σ_d q[h,d]·W[r,h,d]) — different summation order and no intermediate bf16 rounding. Precision: c_kv is bf16, W dequant is ~fp32; fp32 accumulate over 512 or 128 — both fine for cos 0.98. The difference: reference rounds k_nope to bf16. Absorbed form doesn't. bf16 rounding ~1e-2 relative on k_nope which feeds scores ~ this introduces small perturbation of scores; softmax may shift slightly. Cos of OUTPUT hidden should still be ≥ 0.999 in practice. The o-side: reference v rounded to bf16; absorbed o-side: p-weighted c_kv Σ fp32 then W_v GEMV — again fine. Risk assessment: 0.98 is loose (bf16 itself has ~3 decimal digits). The bf16 rounding differences are ~1e-3 relative → cosine ~0.9999. Should be fine. We can sanity check empirically against reference. - MoE: router fp32 softmax, topk, w normalized fp32 then reference out = Σ w_j * ffn fp32 → bf16. Baseline casts w to bf16 — minor. Before diving into a giant CUDA kernel, consider doing it in TRITON. Triton megakernel with spin-barriers... Triton pros: auto pipelining, tl.dot for tensor cores on partials, much faster dev. Cons: atomics-spin barrier less standard; int4 unpack fine (bit ops); dequant GEMV with tl.dot needs materialized bf16 tiles in registers/shared → tl.dot(16×...) — M=1 GEMV doesn't fit tensor cores nicely; use elementwise mul-reduce instead (fine, bandwidth-bound). Barrier: implement arrive-wait via tl.atomic_add on global counter + spin `while tl.load(counter, volatile) < target: pass`. This is used in production triton megakernels (e.g., some MoE kernels). Risk: compiler reordering/volatile semantics; but with tl.atomic ops + tl.load(..., volatile=True) + tl.debug_barrier() it has been done. Custom grid barrier decision: write `gbar()` using 2 counters (sense-reversing): __device__ void gbar(volatile unsigned* cnt, volatile unsigned* gen, int nblocks) { __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { unsigned g = *gen; // ld volatile unsigned t = atomicAdd((unsigned*)cnt, 1u); if (t == nblocks - 1) { *cnt = 0; __threadfence(); atomicAdd((unsigned*)gen, 1u); } else { while (*gen == g) __nanosleep(...); } } __syncthreads(); } Memory ordering: writes before barrier must be visible after: atomicAdd has device scope... need __threadfence() BEFORE arrive (by all writing threads or at least ensure release): pattern: __syncthreads() (block-level), threadfence() by thread 0 covers... other threads' writes: syncthreads orders them before t0's fence? __syncthreads() ensures visibility within block between threads, then t0's __threadfence() + atomic makes them device-visible IF writes were by same thread... NOT strictly (per CUDA docs, threadfence by t0 orders only t0's writes; combined with syncthreads = morphs into block-scope... Common practice (multi-CTA barriers) uses: __threadfence(); __syncthreads(); if(tid0) arrive; ...; __syncthreads(); with the arrive atomic + volatile polling spin — this is the standard "two-phase software barrier" used even in CUDA samples (threadFenceReduction). Reads after barrier: volatile loads bypass L1? Cache coherence: global atomics operate in L2; volatile loads go to L2 (bypass L1? volatile → ld.volatile → yes L2). But REGULAR (non-volatile) writes from other blocks then READS by us after barrier: our regular loads might hit stale L1!! L1 is not coherent across SMs for plain loads. Standard fix: readers must use volatile/atom loads OR writers use __threadfence + readers... In the CUDA sample they rely on... they use `cg::grid_group.sync()`... For hand-rolled: the canonical correct approach (from CUDA docs' threadfence reduction sample): last block writes result then sets flag with threadfence; other blocks volatile-read flag, then read data with... they're reading data written by OTHER blocks earlier: those wrote with plain stores + threadfence before flag; readers read via... sample uses plain reads after volatile flag check — formally requires the flag read to have acquire semantics; combined with writer's release fence, plain loads AFTER acquire are ordered — but L1 caching could serve stale data unless... The data was written AFTER readers' blocks started? Hmm, if the reader block never touched those addresses before, L1 can't have stale lines. In our megakernel, blocks RE-read the same buffers every phase — stale L1 lines from an earlier phase are REAL risk (L1 hit on line cached in phase 3, modified in phase 5 by another SM). Mitigations: use `ld.global.cg` (L2-only, L1 bypass) for all cross-phase shared data reads... impractical to blanket-apply (perf cost on shared-heavy inner loops? .cg fine actually — GEMV x reads from SHARED anyway; weight reads are non-shared per-warp... weight data is read-only! States S/caches written by the same... S written/read by same block? no—task partitioning shifts between phases!). SIMPLEST SAFE RULE: all inter-block-communicated buffers accessed via explicitly uncached/volatile or atomic semantics; OR use cooperative groups grid.sync() which inserts proper memory barriers (grid.sync does release/acquire correctly — that's the whole point of cooperative launch; it handles L1 etc.? grid.sync is documented to synchronize and provide memory ordering across the grid: "synchronizes the entire grid; memory operations before are visible after". YES — that's guaranteed coherency (compiler inserts appropriate fences; hardware-wise, since L1 is per-SM write-through? On Volta+ L1 is write-through?? Not exactly but NVIDIA guarantees grid.sync visibility). USE cooperative grid.sync() — correct-by-construction, ~overhead similar to hand-rolled (it's implemented via atomics too, driver-optimized). Let me write GEMV helper: ```cpp // Quant GEMV partial: computes partial dot for out[n0+lane*16 ..+16) over k in [k0, k0+256) // x: device fp32 (staged? we use shared) — pass float* xs (shared) // W packed uint8 K/2 x N row-major; s,z bf16 (K/128, N) __device__ inline void gemv4_partial( const uint8_t* __restrict__ wq, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ sc, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ zz, const float* __restrict__ xs, // shared, fp32 [K] float* __restrict__ slot, // global fp32 [N] for this chunk int K, int N, int n0 /*=lane base*/, int kg0 /*group start*/, int kgroups) { int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int n = n0 + lane*16; float acc[16] = {0}; if (n < N) { for (int g = 0; g < kgroups; ++g) { int grp = kg0 + g; // load scales/zeros for 16 n float sv[16], szv[16]; ... load from sc[grp*N + n] as bf16 pairs?? 16 bf16 = 32B: two uint4 loads → unpack 16 bf16 → fp32. unpack: __nv_bfloat162 conversions. int kb0 = grp*64; // packed row start for group for (int r = 0; r < 64; ++r) { // packed rows in group uint4 wb = ldg(wq + (kb0+r)*N + n); // 16 bytes = 16 n float x0 = xs[2*(kb0+r)], x1 = xs[2*(kb0+r)+1]; const unsigned char* pb = (unsigned char*)&wb; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 16; ++j) { float lo = (float)(pb[j] & 0xF), hi = (float)(pb[j] >> 4); acc[j] += (lo - zv[j]) * sv[j] * x0 + (hi - zv[j]) * sv[j] * x1; } } } // write slot float4* sp = (float4*)(slot + n); sp[0] = make_float4(acc0..3); sp[1] = ...; sp[2]; sp[3]; } } ``` Per-row inner: 16 bytes → 32 nib: use uchar access (pb[j]) — compiler generates byte extracts from registers — ok. Let me also write a quick mental template for device helpers: ```cpp struct BF2 { float a, b; }; __device__ __forceinline__ float bf2f(bf16 v){ return __bfloat162float(v); } __device__ __forceinline__ float bf16r(float v){ return __bfloat162float(__float2bfloat16(v)); } // round-trip ``` RMS to shared: ```cpp __device__ void stage_rms(const bf16* x, const bf16* w, float* xs, float* red) { // red: shared[32] int tid = threadIdx.x; float ss = 0.f; for (int k = tid; k < D; k += blockDim.x) { float xv = bf2f(x[k]); xs[k] = xv; ss += xv*xv; } // block reduce red[warp] = warpreduce(ss); __syncthreads(); ... float r = rsqrtf(total/D + EPS); for (int k = tid; k < D; k += blockDim.x) xs[k] = bf16r(xs[k]*r*bf2f(w[k])); __syncthreads(); } ``` Note: bf2f(x) loads raw x first (unrounded) then xs = round(xv·r·w) ✓ per ref. gemv partial over units: define ```cpp __device__ inline void gemv4m(const uint8_t* wq, const bf16* sc, const bf16* zz, const float* xs, float* slotbase /*+n*/, int N, int K, int n0, int kc /*chunk idx*/, int KCH /*chunks*/) { // k-chunk: groups [kc*2, kc*2+2) int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int n = n0 + lane*16; if (n >= N) return; float acc[16]; #pragma unroll for (int j=0;j<16;++j) acc[j]=0.f; int g0 = kc*2, g1 = min(g0+2, K/128); for (int g=g0; g<g1; ++g) { float sv[16], zv[16]; // load 16 bf16 scales: sc[g*N + n .. +16) → 32B → two uint4 load_bf16x16(sc + (size_t)g*N + n, sv); load_bf16x16(zz + (size_t)g*N + n, zv); int kb0 = g*64; const uint8_t* wp = wq + (size_t)kb0*N + n; #pragma unroll 8 for (int r=0; r<64; ++r) { uint4 wb = __ldg((const uint4*)(wp + (size_t)r*N)); float x0 = xs[2*(kb0+r)], x1 = xs[2*(kb0+r)+1]; const unsigned char* pb = (const unsigned char*)&wb; #pragma unroll for (int j=0;j<16;++j) { float lo = (float)(pb[j]&15), hi = (float)(pb[j]>>4); acc[j] += (lo - zv[j])*sv[j]*x0 + (hi - zv[j])*sv[j]*x1; } } ... } // vector write float4 v0..: writes slot + n } ``` math opt: precompute per byte: (lo−z)s x0 + (hi−z)s x1 = s·(lo·x0 + hi·x1) − sz·(x0+x1); where sz = zv·sv. per row: t = x0+x1 (1 add); per j: u = lo·x0 + hi·x1 (2 fma) then a = fma(u, sv_j, acc_j) — wait then minus: acc = fma(-sz_j, t, fma(u, sv_j, acc_j)) → 4 FMA per j + 2 I2F... vs original: per j: (lo−z): sub, ×s: mul, ×x0: mul+add via FMA( (lo−z)·s, x0, acc) — precompute per group per j: szv? Actually precompute NOTHING per row: per j: t1 = (lo−zv_j)·sv_j (2 ops) → acc = fma(t1, x0, acc); t2 = (hi−zv_j)·sv_j (2 ops) → acc = fma(t2, x1, acc) → 4 mul/sub + 2 fma + 2 i2f per j. Alternative decomposition: acc_lo[j] += lo·sx0? sx0 depends on row (x0) → per row per j: 2 mul to scale s by x → 32 muls + 32 fma... similar. I'll write it out: ```cpp __device__ __forceinline__ float bf2f(const __nv_bfloat16 v) { return __bfloat162float(v); } __device__ __forceinline__ float bfr(float v) { return __bfloat162float(__float2bfloat16(v)); } __device__ __forceinline__ float warp_sum(float v) { #pragma unroll for (int o=16;o>0;o>>=1) v += __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffffu, v, o); return v; } ``` stage_rms: ```cpp __device__ void stage_rms(const __nv_bfloat16* x, const __nv_bfloat16* w, float* xs, float* red) { const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int lane = tid & 31, wr = tid >> 5; float ss = 0.f; for (int k = tid; k < D; k += blockDim.x) { float xv = bf2f(x[k]); xs[k] = xv; ss += xv*xv; } ss = warp_sum(ss); if (lane == 0) red[wr] = ss; __syncthreads(); if (wr == 0) { float t = (lane < (int)(blockDim.x>>5)) ? red[lane] : 0.f; t = warp_sum(t); if (lane==0) red[0] = t; } __syncthreads(); float r = rsqrtf(red[0] / (float)D + EPS); for (int k = tid; k < D; k += blockDim.x) xs[k] = bfr(xs[k] * r * bf2f(w[k])); __syncthreads(); } ``` BF16 x loads: x[k] scalar bf16 loads — 2304 per block, fine (could vectorize, meh). Aux reduce after staging: for stage_from_obuf (rounding): xs[k] = bfr(obuf[k]). gemv4m — partial over k-chunk (2 groups), n-chunk (lane×16): ```cpp __device__ __forceinline__ void gemv4m(const uint8_t* __restrict__ wq, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ sc, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ zz, const float* __restrict__ xs, float* __restrict__ slot, int N, int g0) { const int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; const int n = (threadIdx.x & ~31) * 0 + lane*16; // n computed outside actually } ``` Hmm — n0 (n-chunk base) must be a parameter. +"""Kimi-Linear W4A16 hybrid decode -- single-launch fused megakernel.
+
+The whole per-token forward (3x KDA + 1x MLA attention, 4x MoE, RMSNorms,
+residual adds, KDA recurrent-state update, MLA latent-cache append) is fused
+into ONE cooperative CUDA kernel launch. int4 dequant is fused into every GEMV:
+packed nibbles are streamed once and dequantized in registers (group-128
+asymmetric, exactly the reference format), never materialized to bf16.
+
+Structure per layer: GEMV phases write k-partial slots (non-atomic), tiny
+reduce phases combine slots into bf16-rounded activations (matching reference
+rounding points), state/attention phases do the KDA recurrence and the
+flash-decoding MLA attention with kv_b absorbed into the latent space (no
+materialized [L, H, D] k/v -- the latent cache is read twice, not expanded).
… +1026 more lines

Implement sense-reversal: ```cpp __device__ inline void gbar(volatile unsigned* cnt, volatile unsigned* gen, unsigned nb) { __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { unsigned g = ld_volatile(gen); __threadfence(); // make prior writes visible before arrival unsigned a = atomicAdd((unsigned*)cnt, 1u); if (a == nb - 1) { *cnt = 0; __threadfence(); atomicAdd((unsigned*)gen, 1u); } else { int backoff = 1; while (ld_volatile(gen) == g) { __nanosleep(32); // tight } } } __syncthreads(); // ensure all threads see post-barrier data: acquire via the volatile gen read + threadfence } ``` Memory ordering correctness: - Writer (each block's t0): __threadfence() BEFORE atomicAdd(cnt) → its block's prior writes are visible device-wide before the arrival increment (fences order own-thread ops; other threads of block are ordered by __syncthreads() before... need __syncthreads() BEFORE t0's fence: __syncthreads() ensures block threads' writes are visible to t0... visibility to t0 only. Then t0's threadfence pushes t0's... hmm, does __threadfence by t0 order OTHER THREADS' writes? NO — formally __threadfence orders only the calling thread's memory ops. BUT combined with __syncthreads(), which "waits until all global/shared writes by threads in the block are visible to the block": NOT device-wide. The canonical safe pattern from CUDA samples (threadFenceReduction): each block: __threadfence() is called by each thread? In the sample: after writing partial results, they call __threadfence(); then __syncthreads(); then tid0 atomicInc ticket; wait. The __threadfence() by ALL THREADS BEFORE syncthreads covers their own writes. I.e.: ``` __threadfence(); // every thread: my writes visible device-wide __syncthreads(); // order all fence+writes before t0's arrival if (tid == 0) { arrive; spin } __syncthreads(); // others wait ``` For the READ side (acquire): after waiting t0 does... the spinning read of gen via ld.volatile returns new value only after release → data is visible IF reads also bypass L1: subsequent NORMAL loads by reader threads may hit STALE L1 lines!! The observed coherence protocol: L1 is not coherent for READS of data written by another SM AFTER the line was cached. If line not present, fetch from L2 gets fresh. Given phases: block reads a buffer in phase i (caches some lines), another block WRITES it in phase i+1, reader in phase i+2 could hit stale L1. The SASS shows a problem: variant B. The loads ARE getting issued in groups (LDG.E.128 CONSTANT at 0x280, 0x2b0, ...) — 128-bit loads appear roughly every ~2 rows interleaved... but WAIT. Interesting: `LDG.E.128.CONSTANT R20, desc[UR6][R24.64+-0x4000]` — those offsets -0x4000 (16KB) — hmm, address math... And note `LDG.E R55/R44/R38/R42` at 0x290-0x2d0 — those are 32-bit loads (x0,x1 from xs... wait xs was GLOBAL in isoB (`const float* xs` arg) — those four 32-bit loads are the x values pre-fetched per group? Those come with negative offsets on R26 (small negative offsets -0x20..-0x14?? Those look like they're loading xs[2*(kb0)] — weird offsets). BUT the smoking gun: **the dependency**: Look at the LDG.128 at 0x280 loading into R20, used IMMEDIATELY by LOP3 at 0x2e0, 0x2f0... Only ~4 instructions between the load and first use!! The compiler did NOT hoist/prefetch loads deeply. Each row's LOP3/I2F chain consumes R20-R23 right after issuing the next load or two. So loads are nearly serialized: issue LDG r, then work on LDG r-1's data... per-iteration latency ≈ DRAM latency (~600-800ns)?? But with 2-3 loads in flight it'd be ~250ns/row... 64 rows = 16-38µs... hmm measured 62µs for 72 units ~ in range. So the core issue: **ptxas is not enough loads in flight** because register pressure at 128 regs caps how many uint4 loads can be outstanding. variant B uses... The fundamental tension: 16 accumulators + 16 unit-floats being processed in a fat unrolled body per 16B load; ptxas can't hide DRAM latency without more outstanding loads. Solutions in order of expected impact: 1. **Process MORE bytes per load-use round trip per lane**: instead of lane covering 16 n (16B per row), have lane cover 32 n (2×16B/row) or 64 n — no wait, that increases register/ALU per load... The key ratio: DRAM latency 600ns per round trip; to sustain 1.5TB/s, each SM needs 600ns×8GB/s ≈ 5KB outstanding per SM = 78 × 64B sectors... per warp: with 16 warps/SM: ~5 sectors = 320B = 20 × 16B loads outstanding per warp. The barrier: outstanding loads per warp are limited by REGISTER budget (buffers for load results). uint4 = 4 regs → 20 loads = 80 regs JUST buffers. 2. **NVDIA-standard trick: use cp.async (memcpy_async) to shared memory!** cp.async bypasses registers: async copy stage into shared, then compute from shared. Outstanding = stages in flight (shared memory is the buffer, ~ up to 8-16 stages of e.g. 1KB). This is how marlin/AWQ kernels achieve BW. Also LDGSTS on sm_120 ✓ (`cp.async.cg.shared.global` 16B). 3. Restructure GEMV so each warp loads a BIG CONTIGUOUS run (not strided rows): The weight layout (K/2, N) row-major means a (kc, nc512) unit reads 64 rows × 512B with 4KB stride. If instead a unit were (n-chunk 128B, whole k range 1152 rows) sequential addresses stride N... still strided. TRUE contiguity impossible with this layout per unit, BUT the warp can simply iterate with wider stride but deeper pipelining via cp.async into a ring buffer: queue depth 16 rows → 16 KB shared/warp?? hmm 16 warps × ring of 8 rows × 512B = 64KB/block — fits. Actually — cp.async is per-thread up to 16B per op; a warp can issue cp.async for a whole 512B row: 32 lanes × 16B → ONE row per instruction-wave. Ring: warp issues rows r..r+7 into shared ring slots, then processes oldest. Shared-to-reg loads via LDS (low latency, hidden). 4. Alternative without shared: **software pipeline with explicit double-buffer regs**: load row r+1's uint4 BEFORE processing row r's. Per-lane: unroll 2 with 2 uint4 buffers (8 regs): latency per row = max(processing time ~130 instr ≈ 40-60 cyc, DRAM latency/queuedepth...) — with only 2 buffers, latency-bound at 300ns/row — NOT enough. Need ~8-16 deep → 32-64 regs buffers — with 128-reg budget and acc/sv/zv ~60 regs, tight. That's what ptxas tried and half-failed. 5. **Change the parallel decomposition to put the k-loop INSIDE so that consecutive 16B loads go to SHARED via cp.async automatically... same as 2. 6. **Vectorize x-usage: note processing 2 packed rows at once needs x0..x3 (4 x values) but enables the prmt-based unpack+float2 trick... doesn't change memory. DECISION: redesign gemv inner loop around **cp.async ring buffer into shared**: - Ring of R stages (say 6), each stage = one packed row per warp (512B): shared per warp = 6×512B = 3KB → 16 warps × 3KB = 48KB/block ✓ (or process 2 rows/stage → 1KB/stage, 8 stages = 8KB/warp, 128KB... too much. 4 stages of 2 rows = 4KB/warp = 64KB/block ok-ish). - Loop: for r: cp.async row (r + lookahead) → commit; wait for stage r; barrier... cp.async wait is per-group: `cp.async.wait_group N-lookahead`. Then LDS-read the row + x into regs, dequant-FMA. - LDS traffic: 512B/row/warp — LDS BW 128B/cyc/SM... per SM 16 warps × (512+64)/row-time... fine. Also x values: broadcast loads currently from SHARED xs — fine (stays). Actually — wait, even simpler and very effective on modern archs: **`__pipeline` prefetch via L2 prefetch hints?** No. cp.async it is.
